<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#d5d5ff;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Abuse" title="Abuse">Abuse</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Close_relationships" title="Template:Close relationships"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Close_relationships" title="Template talk:Close relationships"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Close_relationships&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Divorce</b>, also known as <b>dissolution of marriage</b>, is the process of terminating a <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> or marital union.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Divorce usually entails the canceling or reorganizing of the legal duties and responsibilities of marriage, thus dissolving the <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">bonds of matrimony</a> between a married couple under the rule of law of the particular country or state. Divorce laws <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">vary considerably</a> around the world,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> but in most countries divorce requires the sanction of a court or other authority in a legal process, which may involve issues of <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_property" class="mw-redirect" title="Distribution of property">distribution of property</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica2_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica2-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Child_custody" title="Child custody">child custody</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica2_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica2-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alimony" title="Alimony">alimony</a> (spousal support), <a href="/wiki/Child_visitation_/_access" class="mw-redirect" title="Child visitation / access">child visitation / access</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parenting_time" title="Parenting time">parenting time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_support" title="Child support">child support</a>, and division of debt. In most countries, <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a> is required by law, so divorce allows each former partner to marry another person.
</p><p>Divorce is different from <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a>, which declares the marriage null and void, with <a href="/wiki/Legal_separation" title="Legal separation">legal separation</a> or <i>de jure</i> separation (a legal process by which a married couple may formalize a <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/Marital_separation" title="Marital separation">separation</a> while remaining legally married) or with <i>de facto separation</i> (a process where the spouses informally stop cohabiting). Reasons for divorce vary, from sexual incompatibility or lack of independence for one or both spouses to a <a href="/wiki/Personality_clash" title="Personality clash">personality clash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only countries that do not allow divorce are the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> and the British <a href="/wiki/Crown_Dependency" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Dependency">Crown Dependency</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sark" title="Sark">Sark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In the Philippines, divorce for non-Muslim Filipinos is not legal unless the husband or wife is an alien and satisfies certain conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Vatican City is an ecclesiastical state, which has no procedure for divorce. Countries that have relatively recently legalized divorce are <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (1970), <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (1975), <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> (1977), <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (1981), <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> (1987),<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> (1991),<sup id="cite_ref-State_pp_102_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-State_pp_102-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> (1991*<sup id="cite_ref-State_pp_102_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-State_pp_102-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> (1995),<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> (1996), <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> (2004)<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> (2011).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Grounds for divorce vary widely from country to country. Marriage may be seen as a <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contract</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Personal_status" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal status">status</a>, or a combination of these.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Where it is seen as a contract, the refusal or inability of one spouse to perform the obligations stipulated in the contract may constitute a <a href="/wiki/Ground_for_divorce" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground for divorce">ground for divorce</a> for the other spouse. In contrast, in some countries (such as Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Finland,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Australia,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> New Zealand),<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> divorce is purely <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no fault</a>. Many jurisdictions offer both the option of a <i>no fault</i> divorce as well as an <i>at fault</i> divorce. This is the case, for example, in many <a href="/wiki/US_states" class="mw-redirect" title="US states">US states</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Grounds_for_divorce_(United_States)" title="Grounds for divorce (United States)">Grounds for divorce (United States)</a>).
</p><p>Though divorce laws vary between <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdictions</a>, there are two basic approaches to divorce: fault based and no-fault based. However, even in some jurisdictions that do not require a party to claim fault of their partner, a court may still take into account the behavior of the parties when dividing property, debts, evaluating custody, shared care arrangements and support. In some jurisdictions one spouse may be forced to pay the attorney's fees of another spouse.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Laws vary as to the waiting period before a divorce is effective. Also, residency requirements vary. However, issues of division of property are typically determined by the law of the jurisdiction in which the property is located.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Europe, divorce laws differ from country to country, reflecting differing legal and cultural traditions. In some countries, particularly (but not only) in some former communist countries, divorce can be obtained only on one single general ground of "irretrievable breakdown of the marriage" (or a similar formulation). Yet, what constitutes such a "breakdown" of the marriage is interpreted very differently from jurisdiction to jurisdiction, ranging from very liberal interpretations (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> to quite restrictive ones (e.g., in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, there must be an "irretrievable and complete disintegration of matrimonial life," but there are many restrictions to granting a divorce).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Separation constitutes a ground of divorce in some European countries (in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, e.g., a divorce is granted on the basis of a 1-year separation if both spouses consent, or 3-year separation if only one spouse consents).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Note that "separation" does not necessarily mean separate residences – in some jurisdictions, living in the same household but leading a separate life (e.g., eating, sleeping, socializing, etc. separately) is sufficient to constitute <i>de facto</i> separation; this is explicitly stated, e.g., in the family laws of Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Divorce laws are not static; they often change reflecting evolving social norms of societies. In the 21st century, many European countries have made changes to their divorce laws, in particular by reducing the length of the necessary periods of separation, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_Scotland" title="Divorce in Scotland">Scotland</a> in 2006 (1 or 2 years from the previous 2 or 5 years); <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country#France" title="Divorce law by country">France</a> in 2005 (2 years from the previous 6 years),<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> in 2005 (2 years from the previous 4 years),<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country#Greece" title="Divorce law by country">Greece</a> in 2008 (two years from the previous four years).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Some countries have completely overhauled their divorce laws, such as <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country#Portugal" title="Divorce law by country">Portugal in 2008</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_Belgium" title="Divorce in Belgium">new divorce law</a> also came into force in September 2007 in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, creating a new system that is primarily no-fault.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country#Bulgaria" title="Divorce law by country">Bulgaria</a> also modified its divorce regulations in 2009. Also in <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country#Italy" title="Divorce law by country">Italy</a>, new laws came into force in 2014 and 2015 with significant changes in Italian law in matter of divorce: apart from shortening of the period of obligatory separation (6 months or 1 year from the previous 3 years), are allowed other forms of getting a divorce – as an alternative to court proceedings, i.e. the negotiations with the participation of an advocate or agreement made before the registrar of Public Registry Office.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, instead, is a European country where the divorce law still remains conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The liberalization of divorce laws is not without opposition, particularly in the United States. Indeed, in the US, certain conservative and religious organizations are lobbying for laws which restrict divorce. In 2011, in the US, the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_for_Divorce_Reform" title="Coalition for Divorce Reform">Coalition for Divorce Reform</a> was established, describing itself as an organization "dedicated to supporting efforts to reduce unnecessary divorce and promote healthy marriages."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">magisterium</a> of the Roman Catholic Church founds the concept of marriage on <a href="/wiki/Natural_law#Christianity" title="Natural law">natural moral law</a>, elaborated by St. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, supplemented by the revealed <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">Divine law</a>. The doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor Angelicus</a> has been partially shared by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> in the course of history.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">Divorce law by country</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some jurisdictions, the courts will seldom apply principles of fault, but might willingly hold a party liable for a breach of a fiduciary duty to his or her spouse (for example, see Family Code Sections 720 and 1100 of the California Family Code). Grounds for divorce differs from state to state in the U.S. Some states have <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault divorce</a>; some states require a declaration of fault on the part of one partner or both; some states allow either method.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In most jurisdictions, a divorce must be certified (or ordered by a Judge) by a court of law to come into effect. The terms of the divorce are usually determined by the courts, though they may take into account prenuptial agreements or post-nuptial agreements, or simply ratify terms that the spouses may have agreed to privately (this is not true in the United States, where agreements related to the marriage typically have to be rendered in writing to be enforceable). In absence of agreement, a contested divorce may be stressful to the spouses.
</p><p>In some other countries,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2012)">where?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> when the spouses agree to divorce and to the terms of the divorce, it can be certified by a non-judiciary administrative entity. The effect of a divorce is that both parties are free to marry again if a filing in an appellate court does not overturn the decision.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contested_divorce">Contested divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Contested divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Contested divorces mean that one of several issues are required to be heard by a judge at trial level—this is more expensive, and the parties will have to pay for a lawyer's time and preparation. In such a divorce the spouses are not able to agree on issues for instance child custody and division of marital assets. In such situations, the litigation process takes longer to conclude.<sup id="cite_ref-DSA_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSA-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The judge controls the outcome of the case. Less adversarial approaches to <a href="/wiki/Divorce_settlement" title="Divorce settlement">divorce settlements</a> have recently emerged, such as mediation and collaborative divorce settlement, which negotiate mutually acceptable resolution to conflicts. This principle in the United States is called 'Alternative Dispute Resolution' and has gained popularity.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="At-fault_divorce">At-fault divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: At-fault divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Before the late 1960s, nearly all countries that permitted divorce required proof by one party that the other party had committed an act incompatible to the marriage. This was termed "grounds" for divorce (popularly called "fault") and was the only way to terminate a marriage. Most jurisdictions around the world still require such proof of fault. In the United States, no-fault divorce is available in all 50 states, as is the case with Australia, New Zealand, Canada and other Western countries.
</p><p>Fault-based divorces can be contested; evaluation of offenses may involve allegations of <a href="/wiki/Collusion" title="Collusion">collusion</a> of the parties (working together to get the divorce), or <a href="/wiki/Condonation" title="Condonation">condonation</a> (approving the offense), <a href="/wiki/Connivance" title="Connivance">connivance</a> (tricking someone into committing an offense), or <a href="/wiki/Provocation_(legal)" title="Provocation (legal)">provocation</a> by the other party. Contested fault divorces can be expensive, and not usually practical as eventually most divorces are granted. Comparative rectitude is a doctrine used to determine which spouse is more at fault when both spouses are guilty of breaches.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The grounds for a divorce which a party could raise and need to prove included 'desertion,' 'abandonment,' 'cruelty,' or 'adultery.' The requirement of proving a ground was revised (and withdrawn) by the terms of 'no-fault' statutes, which became popular in many Western countries in the late 1960s and early 1970s. In 'no-fault' jurisdictions divorce can be obtained either on a simple allegation of 'irreconcilable differences,' 'irretrievable break-down', or 'incompatibility' with respect to the marriage relationship, or on the ground of <span class="nowrap">de facto</span> separation.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary_divorce">Summary divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Summary divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A summary (or simple) divorce, available in some jurisdictions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2016)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, is used when spouses meet certain eligibility requirements or can agree on key issues beforehand.
</p><p>Key factors:
</p>
<ul><li>Short duration of marriage (less than five years)</li>
<li>Absence of children (or, in some jurisdictions, prior allocation of child custody and of child-support direction and amount)</li>
<li>Absence or minimal value of real property at issue and any associated encumbrances such as mortgages</li>
<li>Absence of agreed-as-marital property above a given value threshold (around $35,000 not including vehicles)</li>
<li>Absence, with respect to each spouse, of claims to personal property above a given value threshold, typically the same as that for total marital property, with such claims including claims to exclusive previous ownership of property described by the other spouse as marital</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="No-fault_divorce">No-fault divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: No-fault divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some Western jurisdictions have a <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault divorce</a> system, which requires no allegation or proof of fault of either party.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The barest of assertions suffice. For example, in countries that require "irretrievable breakdown", the mere assertion that the marriage has broken down will satisfy the judicial officer. In other jurisdictions requiring irreconcilable differences, the mere allegation that the marriage has been irreparable by these differences is enough for granting a divorce. Courts will not inquire into facts. A "yes" is enough, even if the other party vehemently says "no".
</p><p>The application can be made by either party or by both parties jointly.
</p><p>In jurisdictions adopting the 'no-fault' principle regarding whether to <i>grant</i> a divorce, some courts may still take into account the fault of the parties when determining some aspects of the <i>content</i> of the divorce decree, <i>e.g.</i>, its terms for the division of property and debts and the existence and, if applicable, the amount of spousal support. Provisions related to child custody are determined using a different fundamental standard, that of the child's or children's best interests; while some behaviors that may constitute marital fault (<i>e.g.</i>, violence, cruelty, endangerment, neglect, or substance abuse) may also qualify as factors to be considered when determining child custody, they do so for the independent reason that they provide evidence as to what arrangement is in the child's or children's best interests going forward.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uncontested_divorce">Uncontested divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Uncontested divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It is estimated that upwards of 95% of divorces in the U.S. are "uncontested",<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> because the two parties are able to come to an agreement (either with or without lawyers/mediators/collaborative counsel) about the property, children, and support issues. When the parties can agree and present the court with a fair and equitable agreement, approval of the divorce is almost guaranteed. If the two parties cannot come to an agreement, they may ask the court to decide how to split property and deal with the custody of their children. Though this may be necessary, the courts would prefer parties come to an agreement prior to entering court.
</p><p>Where the issues are not complex and the parties are cooperative, a settlement often can be directly negotiated between them. In the majority of cases, forms are acquired from their respective state websites and a filing fee is paid to the state.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Most U.S. states charge between $175 and $350 for a simple divorce filing.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Collaborative_divorce" class="mw-redirect" title="Collaborative divorce">Collaborative divorce</a> and mediated divorce are considered uncontested divorces.
</p><p>Because of additional requirements that must be met, most <a href="/wiki/Military_divorce" title="Military divorce">military divorces</a> are typically uncontested.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, many <a href="/wiki/State_court_(United_States)" title="State court (United States)">state court</a> systems are experiencing an increasing proportion of <i><a href="/wiki/Pro_se" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro se">pro se</a></i> (<i>i.e.,</i> litigants represent themselves without a lawyer) in divorce cases.<sup id="cite_ref-prose_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prose-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In San Diego, for example, the number of divorce filings involving at least one self-representing litigant rose from 46% in 1992 to 77% in 2000, and in Florida from 66% in 1999 to 73% in 2001. Urban courts in California report that approximately 80% of the new divorce filings are filed <i>pro se.</i><sup id="cite_ref-trends_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trends-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborative_divorce">Collaborative divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Collaborative divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Collaborative_divorce" class="mw-redirect" title="Collaborative divorce">Collaborative divorce</a> is a method for divorcing couples to come to agreement on divorce issues. In a collaborative divorce, the parties negotiate an agreed resolution with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">attorneys</a> who are trained in the collaborative divorce process and in mediation and often with the assistance of a neutral financial specialist or divorce coaches. The parties are empowered to make their own decisions based on their own needs and interests, but with complete information and full professional support.
</p><p>Once the collaborative divorce starts, the lawyers are disqualified from representing the parties in a contested legal proceeding, should the <a href="/wiki/Collaborative_law" title="Collaborative law">collaborative law</a> process end prematurely. Most attorneys who practice collaborative divorce claim that it can be more cost-effective than other divorce methods, <i>e.g.,</i> going to court.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Expense, they say, has to be looked at under the headings of financial and emotional. Also, the experience of working collaboratively tends to improve communication between the parties, particularly when collaborative coaches are involved, and the possibility of going back to court post-separation or divorce is minimized. In the course of the collaboration, should the parties not reach any agreements, any documents or information exchanged during the collaborative process cannot be used in court except by agreement between the parties.
</p><p>Neither can any of the professional team retained in the course of the collaboration be brought to court. Essentially, they have the same protections as in mediation. There are two exceptions: 1) Any affidavit sworn in the course of the collaboration and vouching documentation attaching to same and 2) any interim agreement made and signed off in the course of the collaboration or correspondence relating thereto. The parties are in control of the time they are prepared to give their collaboration. Some people need a lot of time to complete, whereas others will reach solutions in a few meetings. Collaborative practitioners offer a tightly orchestrated model with meetings scheduled in advance every two weeks, and the range of items to be discussed apportioned in advance of signing up as well as the more open ended process, the clients decide.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic_divorce">Electronic divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Electronic divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, for example, allows two persons to file an <a href="/wiki/Computer_file" title="Computer file">electronic</a> request for <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault</a> <a href="/wiki/Collaborative_divorce" class="mw-redirect" title="Collaborative divorce">collaborative divorce</a> in a non <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a> <a href="/wiki/Administration_(government)" title="Administration (government)">administrative</a> <a href="/wiki/Legal_entity" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal entity">entity</a>. In specific cases, with no <a href="/wiki/Children" class="mw-redirect" title="Children">children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Real_property" title="Real property">real property</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alimony" title="Alimony">alimony</a>, or common address, can be completed within one hour.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Emery_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emery-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediated_divorce">Mediated divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mediated divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Divorce mediation is an alternative to traditional divorce litigation. In a divorce mediation session, a mediator facilitates the discussion between the two parties by assisting with communication and providing information and suggestions to help resolve differences. At the end of the mediation process, the separating parties have typically developed a tailored divorce agreement that can be submitted to the court. Mediation sessions can include either party's attorneys, a neutral attorney, or an attorney-mediator who can inform both parties of their legal rights, but does not provide advice to either, or can be conducted with the assistance of a facilitative or transformative mediator without attorneys present at all. Some mediation companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Wevorce" title="Wevorce">Wevorce</a>, also pair clients with counselors, financial planners and other professionals to work through common mediation sticking points.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Divorce mediators may be attorneys who have experience in divorce cases, or they may be professional mediators who are not attorneys, but who have training specifically in the area of family court matters. Divorce mediation can be significantly less costly, both financially and emotionally, than litigation. The adherence rate to mediated agreements is much higher than that of adherence to court orders.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> An article in the Jerusalem Post by Hadassah Fidler explained that mediated divorces have become a lot more popular, to the extent that some countries (such as Israel) have instituted a new law which will require divorcing couples to consider mediation before applying to court.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Polygamy_and_divorce">Polygamy and divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Polygamy and divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">Polygamy</a> is a significant structural factor governing divorce in countries where this is permitted. Little-to-no analysis has been completed to explicitly explain the link between marital instability and polygamy which leads to divorce. The frequency of divorce rises in polygamous marriages compared to monogamous relationships. Within polygamous unions, differences in conjugal stability are found to occur by wife order. There are 3 main mechanisms through which polygamy affects divorce: economic restraint, sexual satisfaction, and childlessness. Many women escape economic restraint through divorcing their spouses when they are allowed to initiate a divorce.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Just_divorced.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Just_divorced.jpg/220px-Just_divorced.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Just_divorced.jpg/330px-Just_divorced.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Just_divorced.jpg/440px-Just_divorced.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Just_divorced.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Just Divorced!" hand-written on an automobile's&#160;rear&#160;window.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An annual study in the UK by management consultants <a href="/wiki/Grant_Thornton" class="mw-redirect" title="Grant Thornton">Grant Thornton</a>, estimates the main proximal causes of divorce based on surveys of matrimonial lawyers.<sup id="cite_ref-gt_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gt-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main causes in 2004 were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">Adultery</a>; <a href="/wiki/Extramarital_sex" title="Extramarital sex">Extramarital sex</a>; <a href="/wiki/Infidelity" title="Infidelity">Infidelity</a>&#160;– 27%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">Domestic violence</a>&#160;– 17%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Midlife_crisis" title="Midlife crisis">Midlife crisis</a>&#160;– 13%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Addiction" title="Addiction">Addictions</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Problem_gambling" title="Problem gambling">gambling</a>&#160;– 6%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Workaholic" title="Workaholic">Workaholism</a>&#160;– 6%</li>
<li>Other factors &#160;- 31%</li></ul>
<p>According to this survey, husbands engaged in extramarital affairs in 75% of cases, wives in 25%. In cases of family strain, wives' families were the primary source of strain in 78%, compared to 22% of husbands' families. Emotional and physical abuse were more evenly split, with wives affected in 60% and husbands in 40% of cases. In 70% of workaholism-related divorces it was husbands who were the cause, and in 30%, wives. The 2004 survey found that 93% of divorce cases were petitioned by wives, very few of which were contested. 53% of divorces were of marriages that had lasted 10 to 15 years, with 40% ending after 5 to 10 years. The first 5 years are relatively divorce-free, and if a marriage survives more than 20 years it is unlikely to end in divorce.
</p><p>Social scientists study the causes of divorce in terms of underlying factors that may possibly motivate divorce. One of these factors is the age at which a person gets married; delaying marriage may provide more opportunity or experience in choosing a compatible partner.<sup id="cite_ref-red-v-blue_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-red-v-blue-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Wage, income, and sex ratios are other such underlying factors that have been included in analyses by sociologists and economists.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The elevation of divorce rates among couples who <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabited</a> prior to marriage is called the "cohabitation effect." Evidence suggests that although <a href="/wiki/Correlation_does_not_imply_causation" title="Correlation does not imply causation">this correlation is partly due to two forms of selection</a> (<i>a</i>) that persons whose moral or religious codes permit cohabitation are also more likely to consider divorce permitted by morality or religion and (<i>b</i>) that marriage based on low levels of commitment is more common among couples who cohabit than among couples who do not, such that the mean and median levels of commitment at the start of marriage are lower among cohabiting than among non-cohabiting couples), the cohabitation experience itself exerts at least some independent effect on the subsequent marital union.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, a study by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jay_Teachman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jay Teachman (page does not exist)">Jay Teachman</a> published in <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Marriage_and_Family" title="Journal of Marriage and Family">Journal of Marriage and Family</a></i> found that women who have cohabited or had premarital sex with men other than their husbands have an increased risk of divorce, and that this effect is strongest for women who have cohabited with multiple men prior to marriage. To Teachman, the fact that the elevated risk of divorce is only experienced when the premarital partner(s) is someone other than the husband indicates that premarital sex and cohabitation are now a normal part of the courtship process in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This study only considers data on women in the 1995 National Survey of Family Growth in the United States. Divorce is sometimes caused by one of the partners finding the other <a href="/wiki/Unattractive" class="mw-redirect" title="Unattractive">unattractive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some of the effects associated with divorce include academic, behavioral, and psychological problems. Although this may not always be true, studies suggest that children from divorced families are more likely to exhibit such behavioral issues than those from non-divorced families.<sup id="cite_ref-Family,_Consumer_2003_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family,_Consumer_2003-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divorce_and_relationships">Divorce and relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Divorce and relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Research done at <a href="/wiki/Northern_Illinois_University" title="Northern Illinois University">Northern Illinois University</a> on Family and Child Studies suggests that divorce of couples experiencing high conflict can have a positive effect on families by reducing conflict in the home. There are, however, many instances when the parent–child relationship may suffer due to divorce. Financial support is many times lost when an adult goes through a divorce. The adult may be obligated to obtain additional work to maintain financial stability. In turn, this can lead to a negative relationship between the parent and child; the relationship may suffer due to lack of attention towards the child as well as minimal parental supervision<sup id="cite_ref-Family,_Consumer_2003_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family,_Consumer_2003-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have also shown that parental skills decrease after a divorce occurs; however, this effect is only a temporary change. "A number of researchers have shown that a disequilibrium, including diminished parenting skills, occurs in the year following the divorce but that by two years after the divorce re-stabilization has occurred and parenting skills have improved"<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some couples choose divorce even when one spouse's desire to remain married is greater than the other spouse's desire to obtain a divorce. In economics this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Zelder_Paradox" class="mw-redirect" title="Zelder Paradox">Zelder Paradox</a>, and is more common with marriages that have produced children, and less common with childless couples.<sup id="cite_ref-Zelder1993_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelder1993-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In an <a href="/wiki/American_Psychological_Association" title="American Psychological Association">American Psychological Association</a> study of parents' relocation after a divorce, researchers found that a move has a long-term effect on children. In the first study conducted amongst 2,000 college students on the effects of parental relocation relating to their children's well-being after divorce, researchers found major differences. In divorced families in which one parent moved, the students received less financial support from their parents compared with divorced families in which neither parent moved. These findings also imply other negative outcomes for these students, such as more distress related to the divorce and did not feel a sense of emotional support from their parents. Although the data suggests negative outcomes for these students whose parents relocate after divorce, there is insufficient research that can alone prove the overall well-being of the child<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A newer study in the <i>Journal of Family Psychology</i> found that parents who move more than an hour away from their children after a divorce are much less well off than those parents who stayed in the same location<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_children">Effects on children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Effects on children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological">Psychological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Psychological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Divorce is associated with diminished psychological well-being in children and adult offspring of divorced parents, including greater unhappiness, less satisfaction with life, weaker sense of personal control, anxiety, depression, and greater use of mental health services. A preponderance of evidence indicates that there is a causal effect between divorce and these outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-Amato_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amato-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> led by the Centre for Health Equity Studies (Chess) at Stockholm University/Karolinska Institutet, is published in the <i>Journal of Epidemiology &amp; Community Health</i> found that children living with just one parent after divorce suffer from more problems such as headaches, stomach aches, feelings of tension and sadness than those whose parents share custody.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Children of divorced parents are also more likely to experience conflict in their own marriages, and are more likely to experience divorce themselves. They are also more likely to be involved in short-term cohabiting relationships, which often dissolve before marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-Amato_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amato-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> There are many studies that show proof of an intergenerational transmission of divorce, but this does not mean that having divorced parents will absolutely lead a child to divorce. There are two key factors that make this transmission of divorce more likely. First, inherited biological tendencies or genetic conditions may predispose a child to divorce as well as the "model of marriage" presented by the child's parents.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Wall_(judge)" title="Nicholas Wall (judge)">Nicholas Wall</a>, former <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Family_Division" title="President of the Family Division">President of the Family Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">English High Court</a>, "People think that post-separation parenting is easy&#160;– in fact, it is exceedingly difficult, and as a rule of thumb my experience is that the more intelligent the parent, the more intractable the dispute. There is nothing worse, for most children, than for their parents to denigrate each other. Parents simply do not realize the damage they do to their children by the battles they wage over them. Separating parents rarely behave reasonably, although they always believe that they are doing so, and that the other party is behaving unreasonably."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Children involved in high-conflict divorce or custody cases can experience varying forms of <a href="/wiki/Parental_alienation" title="Parental alienation">parental alienation</a>, which courts often consider to be a form of child abuse. Specific examples of parental alienation include brainwashing the child to cease their relationship with the other parent, telling the child that the other parent does not love them, teaching the child to call another adult by a parental name in effort to replace the other parent, limiting communication between the child and the other parent, and limiting quality time between the child and the other parent. If evidence reveals that a parent is actively alienating the child from their other parent, their case for custody can be severely damaged.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research shows that children can be affected 2–4 years before the separation or divorce even occurs. This can be due to parental conflict and anticipation of a divorce, and decreased parental contact. Many couples believe that by separating, or becoming legally divorced that they are helping their children, and in situations of extreme parental conflict of abuse it most likely will be beneficial.<sup id="cite_ref-Children_2014_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Children_2014-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to marital conflict and instability, most often has negative consequences for children. Several mechanisms are likely to be responsible. First, observing overt conflict between parents is a direct stressor for children.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Observational studies reveal that children react to inter-parental conflict with fear, anger, or the inhibition of normal behavior. Preschool children&#160;– who tend to be egocentric&#160;– may blame themselves for marital conflict, resulting in feelings of guilt and lowered self-esteem. Conflict between parents also tends to spill over and negatively affect the quality of parents' interactions with their children. Researchers found that the associations between marital conflict and children's externalizing and internalizing problems were largely mediated by parents' use of harsh punishment and parent–child conflict. Furthermore, modeling verbal or physical aggression, parents "teach" their children that disagreements are resolved through conflict rather than calm discussion. As a result, children may not learn the social skills (such as the ability to negotiate and reach compromises) that are necessary to form mutually rewarding relationships with peers.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Girls and boys deal with divorce differently. For instance, girls who initially show signs of adapting well, later suffer from anxiety in romantic relationships with men. Studies also showed that girls who were separated from their fathers at a younger age tended to be more angry toward the situation as they aged. Anger and sadness were also observed at common feeling in adolescents who had experienced parental divorce.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_and_socioeconomic">Academic and socioeconomic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Academic and socioeconomic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Frequently, children who have experienced a divorce have lower academic achievement than children from non-divorced families<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
In a review of family and school factors related to adolescents' academic performance, it noted that a child from a divorced family is two times more likely to drop out of high school than a child from a non-divorced family. These children from divorced families may also be less likely to attend college, resulting in the discontinuation of their academic career.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many times academic problems are associated with those children from <a href="/wiki/Single-parent" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-parent">single-parent</a> families. Studies have shown that this issue may be directly related to the economical influence of divorce. A divorce may result in the parent and children moving to an area with a higher poverty rate and a poor education system all due to the financial struggles of a single parent.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Children of divorced parents also achieve lower levels of socioeconomic status, income, and wealth accumulation than children of continuously married parents. These outcomes are associated with lower educational achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-Amato_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amato-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Young men or women between the ages of 7 and 16 who had experienced the divorce of their parents were more likely than youths who had not experienced the divorce of their parents to leave home because of friction, to cohabit before marriage, and to parent a child before marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Divorce often leads to worsened academic achievement in children ages 7–12, the most heightened negative effect being reading test scores. These negative effects tend to persist, and even escalate after the divorce or separation occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-Children_2014_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Children_2014-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Children of divorced or separated parents exhibit increased behavioral problems and the marital conflict that accompanies parents' divorce places the child's social competence at risk.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divorce_of_elderly_couples">Divorce of elderly couples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Divorce of elderly couples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the mid-1990s, the divorce rate has increased to over 50% among <a href="/wiki/Baby_boomer" class="mw-redirect" title="Baby boomer">baby boomers</a>. More and more seniors are staying single; an analysis of census data conducted at <a href="/wiki/Bowling_Green_State_University" title="Bowling Green State University">Bowling Green State University</a> predicted that divorce numbers will continue to rise. Baby boomers that remain unmarried are five times more likely to live in poverty compared to those who are married. They are also three times as likely to receive food stamps, public assistance or disability payments.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sociologists believe that the rise in the number of older Americans who are not married is a result of factors such as longevity and economics. Women, especially, are becoming more and more financially independent which allows them to feel more secure with being alone, in addition to changing perceptions of being divorced or single. This has resulted in less pressure for baby boomers to marry or stay married.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Divorce_demography" title="Divorce demography">Divorce demography</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Japan, divorces were on a generally upward trend from the 1960s until 2002 when they hit a peak of 290,000. Since then, both the number of divorces and the divorce rate declined for six years straight. In 2010, the number of divorces totalled 251,000, and the divorce rate was 1.99 (per 1,000 population).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/The_Hindu_Marriage_Act,_1955" title="The Hindu Marriage Act, 1955">Hindu Marriage Act</a> is an Act of the Parliament of India enacted in 1955. Three other important acts were also enacted as part of the Hindu Code Bills during this time: the Hindu Succession Act (1956), the Hindu Minority and Guardianship Act (1956), the Hindu Adoptions and Maintenance Act (1956).
DIVORCE UNDER VARIOUS ACTS IN INDIA
The Parsi Marriage and Divorce Act, 1936
The dissolution of Muslim Marriage act, 1939
The Parsi Marriage and Divorce Act, 1936
The Special Marriage Act, 1956
The Foreign Marriage Act, 1969
Dissolution of Muslim Marriages Act, 1939
The Dissolution of Marriage and Judicial separation (under the Indian Divorce Act, 1869)
</p><p>Due to the existence of diverse religious faiths in India, the Indian Judiciary has implemented laws separately for couples belonging to different religious beliefs.
Mutual consent divorce procedure <sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> is relatively easier and fast while contested divorce procedure <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> takes longer and depends on the religions of the couples.
</p>
<h4><span id="Taiwan.2FRepublic_of_China"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan/Republic_of_China">Taiwan/Republic of China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Taiwan/Republic of China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2015, there were 53,448 divorces, in which 33% for less than 5 years of marriage and 20.8% for 5–9 years of marriage. The figure represents a 17.1% decline in the number when divorce rate peaked in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One study estimated that legal reforms accounted for about 20% of the increase in divorce rates in Europe between 1960 and 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2015, the ten places with the highest divorce rates in the UK are all beside the sea, with Blackpool in the top position.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
The highest number of divorce applications are reportedly made on Divorce Day, which is always the first Monday of the new year <sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_the_United_States" title="Divorce in the United States">Divorce in the United States</a></div>
<p>In 2012, on average, first marriages that end in divorce last about eight years.<sup id="cite_ref-cens_gov_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cens_gov-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Of the first marriages for women from 1955 to 1959, about 79% marked their 15th anniversary, compared with only 57% for women who married for the first time from 1985 to 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-cens_gov_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cens_gov-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The median time between divorce and a second marriage was about three and a half years.<sup id="cite_ref-cens_gov_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cens_gov-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 study found a 1% increase in the <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment rate">unemployment rate</a> correlated with a 1% decrease in the divorce rate,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> presumably because more people were financially challenged to afford the legal proceedings.
</p><p>In 2010, success in marriage has been associated with higher education and higher age. 81% of college graduates, over 26 years of age, who wed in the 1980s, were still married 20 years later. 65% of college graduates under 26, who married in the 1980s, were still married 20 years later. 49% of high school graduates under 26 years old, who married in the 1980s, were still married 20 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> 2.9% of adults age 35–39 without a college degree divorced in the year 2009, compared with 1.6% with a college education.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> A population study found that in 2004 and 2008, liberal-voting states have lower rates of divorce than conservative-voting states, possibly because people in liberal states tend to wait longer before getting married.<sup id="cite_ref-red-v-blue_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-red-v-blue-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> An analysis of this study found it to be misleading due to sampling at an aggregate level. It revealed that when sampling the same data by individuals, Republican-leaning voters are less likely to have a divorce or extramarital affair than Democratic-leaning voters and independents.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, based on studies by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jenifer_L._Bratter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jenifer L. Bratter (page does not exist)">Jenifer L. Bratter</a> and Rosalind B. King made publicly available on the <a href="/wiki/Education_Resources_Information_Center" title="Education Resources Information Center">Education Resources Information Center</a>, unions between white males and non-white females (and between <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanics</a> and non-Hispanic persons) have similar or lower risks of divorce than white-white marriages, unions between white male-black female last longer than white-white pairings or white-Asian pairings. Conversely, white female-black male and white female-Asian male marriages are more prone to divorce than white-white pairings.<sup id="cite_ref-ERIC_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ERIC-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, a study<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> by the Christian poll group <a href="/wiki/The_Barna_Group" title="The Barna Group">the Barna Group</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> reports that a higher divorce rate was associated with infrequent church attendance.
</p><p>In 2001, marriages between people of different faiths were three times more likely to be divorced than those of the same faith. In a 1993 study, members of two mainline Protestant religions had a 20% chance of being divorced in 5 years; a Catholic and an Evangelical, a 33% chance; a Jew and a Christian, a 40% chance.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, the divorce rate reached its peak at 40%; since then it has slowly declined, and by 2014 it had settled in at 32%.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Health_Statistics" title="National Center for Health Statistics">National Center for Health Statistics</a> reports that from 1975 to 1988 in the U.S., in families with children present, wives file for divorce in approximately two-thirds of cases. In 1975, 71.4% of the cases were filed by women, and in 1988, 65% were filed by women.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
It is estimated that upwards of 95% of divorces in the U.S. are "uncontested", because the two parties are able to come to an agreement without a hearing (either with or without lawyers/mediators/collaborative counsel) about the property, children, and support issues.
</p><p>A 1995 study found a wide range of factors correlating with the divorce rate including frequency of sex, wealth, race, and religious commitment.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2016, based on <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.budgetdirect.com.au/blog/marriage-and-divorce-statistics-australia.html">https://www.budgetdirect.com.au/blog/marriage-and-divorce-statistics-australia.html</a> the divorce percent was 0.2%<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, based on <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.abs.gov.au/ausstats/abs@.nsf/Latestproducts/3310.0Main%20Features42017">http://www.abs.gov.au/ausstats/abs@.nsf/Latestproducts/3310.0Main%20Features42017</a> the divorce percent was 5%<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://aifs.gov.au/facts-and-figures/marriage-and-divorce-rates">https://aifs.gov.au/facts-and-figures/marriage-and-divorce-rates</a> the divorce percent is about 5%<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.marieclaire.com.au/divorce-statistics-australia">https://www.marieclaire.com.au/divorce-statistics-australia</a> the divorce percentage is about 2%<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a></b>
</p><p>In 1992, there were 9,114 divorces, and the divorce rate (number of divorces per 1,000 existing marriages) was 11.9 or 1.19%.
</p><p>In 2017, there were 8,001 divorces and the divorce rate (number of divorces per 1,000 existing marriages) was 8.4 or 0.84%.
</p>
<h2><span id="In_same-sex_married_couples_.28United_States.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="In_same-sex_married_couples_(United_States)">In same-sex married couples (United States)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: In same-sex married couples (United States)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Globe icon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" decoding="async" width="48" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this section <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Divorce" title="Talk:Divorce">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2013</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>All U.S. states permit <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_States" title="Same-sex marriage in the United States">same-sex marriage</a>. For same-sex couples in the United States, divorce law is in its infancy.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_of_spouses_to_custody_of_children">Rights of spouses to custody of children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Rights of spouses to custody of children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon dissolution of a same-sex marriage, legal questions remain as to the rights of spouses to custody of the biological children of their spouses.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes1_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Unresolved legal questions abound in this area.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Child custody policies include several guidelines that determine with whom the child lives following divorce, how time is divided in joint custody situations, and visitation rights. The most frequently applied custody guideline is the <i>best interests of the child</i> standard, which takes into account the parents' preferences, the child's preferences, the interactions between parents and children, children's adjustment, and all family members' mental and physical health.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_divorce">Religion and divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Religion and divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_divorce" title="Religion and divorce">Religion and divorce</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 290px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width: 288px; height: px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -4px; left: -6px; width: 300px"><div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_(1906).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Samuel D. Ehrhart&#39;s &quot;An International High Noon Divorce&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg/300px-Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="211" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg/450px-Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg/600px-Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_%281906%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7344" data-file-height="5161" /></a></div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Samuel_D._Ehrhart_-_An_International_High_Noon_Divorce_(1906).jpg" title="File:Samuel D. Ehrhart - An International High Noon Divorce (1906).jpg"> </a></div>Cartoon parodying the circus-like divorce proceedings of <a href="/wiki/Anna_Gould" title="Anna Gould">Anna Gould</a> (an American <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">heiress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialite" title="Socialite">socialite</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Boni_de_Castellane" title="Boni de Castellane">Boni de Castellane</a> (a French <a href="/wiki/Nobleman" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobleman">nobleman</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> in 1906 in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, France.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Boni de Castellane then sought an annulment from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a> so that he could be free to remarry in the Church. The annulment case was not finally settled until 1924, when the highest Vatican tribunal upheld the validity of the marriage and denied the annulment.
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In some countries (commonly in Europe and North America), the government defines and administers marriages and divorces. While ceremonies may be performed by religious officials on behalf of the state, a <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil marriage</a> and thus, civil divorce (without the involvement of a religion) is also possible. Due to differing standards and procedures, a couple can be legally unmarried, married, or divorced by the state's definition, but have a different status as defined by a religious order. Other countries use religious law to administer marriages and divorces, eliminating this distinction. In these cases, religious officials are generally responsible for interpretation and implementation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> allows, yet generally advises against divorce, and <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_Islam" title="Divorce in Islam">it can be initiated by either the husband or the wife</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dharmic_religions" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharmic religions">Dharmic religions</a> allow divorce under some circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_divorce" title="Christian views on divorce">Christian views on divorce</a> vary: Roman Catholic teaching allows only annulment, while most other denominations discourage it except in the event of adultery. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_Wesleyan_Methodist_Connection" title="Allegheny Wesleyan Methodist Connection">Allegheny Wesleyan Methodist Connection</a>, in its 2014 Discipline, teaches:<sup id="cite_ref-AWMC2014_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWMC2014-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We believe that the only legitimate marriage is the joining of one man and one woman (Gen. 2:24; Rom. 7:2; 1 Cor. 7:10; Eph. 5:22, 23). We deplore the evils of divorce and remarriage.
