We regard adultery as the only scripturally justifiable grounds for divorce; and the party guilty of adultery has by his or her act forfeited membership in the church. In the case of divorce for other cause, neither party shall be permitted to marry again during the lifetime of the other; and violation of this law shall be punished by expulsion from the church (Matt. 5:32; Mark 10:11, 12). In the carrying out of these principles, guilt shall be established in accordance with judicial procedures set forth in <i>The Discipline</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-AWMC2014_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AWMC2014-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jewish_views_on_marriage" title="Jewish views on marriage">Jewish views of divorce</a> differ, with Reform Judaism considering civil divorces adequate; Conservative and Orthodox Judaism, on the other hand, require that the husband grant his wife a divorce in the form of a <i><a href="/wiki/Get_(divorce_document)" title="Get (divorce document)">get</a></i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">The Millet System</a>, where each religious group regulates its own marriages and divorces, is still present in varying degrees in some post−Ottoman countries like Iraq, Syria, Jordan, Lebanon, Israel, the Palestinian Authority, Egypt, and Greece. Several countries use <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> (Islamic law) to administrate marriages and divorces for Muslims. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Israel" title="Marriage in Israel">Marriage in Israel</a> is administered separately by each religious community (Jews, Christians, Muslims, and Druze), and there is no provision for interfaith marriages other than marrying in another country. For Jews, marriage and divorce are administered by Orthodox rabbis. Partners can file for divorce either in <a href="/wiki/Rabbinical_court" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbinical court">rabbinical court</a> or Israeli civil court.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_divorce">Gender and divorce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Gender and divorce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to a study published in the American Law and Economics Review, women have filed slightly more than two-thirds of divorce cases in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This trend is mirrored in the UK where a recent study into web search behavior found that 70% of divorce inquiries were from women. These findings also correlate with the Office for National Statistics publication "Divorces in England and Wales 2012<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> which reported that divorce petitions from women outnumber those from men by 2 to 1.
</p><p>Regarding divorce settlements, according to the 2004 Grant Thornton survey in the UK, women obtained a better or considerably better settlement than men in 60% of cases. In 30% of cases the assets were split 50–50, and in only 10% of cases did men achieve better settlements (down from 24% the previous year). The report concluded that the percentage of <a href="/wiki/Shared_residency_in_English_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Shared residency in English law">shared residence</a> orders would need to increase in order for more equitable financial divisions to become the norm.<sup id="cite_ref-gt_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gt-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some jurisdictions give unequal rights to men and women when filing for divorce.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>For couples to Conservative or Orthodox Jewish law (which by <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Israel" title="Marriage in Israel">Israeli civil law</a> includes all Jews in Israel), the husband must grant his wife a divorce through a document called a <i><a href="/wiki/Get_(divorce_document)" title="Get (divorce document)">get</a></i>. Granting the 'get' obligates him to pay the woman a significant sum of money(10,000-$20,000) as stated on the religious prenuptial contract, which can be in addition to whatever prior settlement he had reached as far as continuous child support and funds he had to pay by court order in the civil divorce. If the man refuses, (and agreeing on condition he won't have to pay the money is still called refusing), the woman can appeal to a court or the community to pressure the husband. A woman whose husband refuses to grant the get or a woman whose husband is missing without sufficient knowledge that he died, is called an <a href="/wiki/Agunah" title="Agunah">agunah</a>, is still married, and therefore cannot remarry. Under Orthodox law, children of an extramarital affair involving a married Jewish woman are considered <i>mamzerim</i> (<a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(family_law)" title="Legitimacy (family law)">illegitimate</a>) and cannot marry non-<i>mamzerim</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greco-Roman_culture">Greco-Roman culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Greco-Roman culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Marriage in ancient Rome">Marriage in ancient Rome</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_marriage_vows.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Roman_marriage_vows.jpg/200px-Roman_marriage_vows.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Roman_marriage_vows.jpg/300px-Roman_marriage_vows.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Roman_marriage_vows.jpg/400px-Roman_marriage_vows.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2494" data-file-height="2551" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_marriage_vows.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman married couple.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Athens">ancient Athenians</a> liberally allowed divorce, but the person requesting divorce had to submit the request to a <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">magistrate</a>, and the magistrate could determine whether the reasons given were sufficient.
</p><p>Divorce was rare in early Roman culture but as their <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">empire</a> grew in power and authority <a href="/wiki/Roman_law#civil_law" title="Roman law">Roman civil law</a> embraced the maxim, "<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">matrimonia debent esse libera</i>" ("marriages ought to be free"), and either husband or wife could renounce the marriage at will. The Christian emperors <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodosius" title="Theodosius">Theodosius</a> restricted the grounds for divorce to grave cause, but this was relaxed by Justinian in the 6th century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Europe">Medieval Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Medieval Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the fall of the Roman Empire, familial life was regulated more by ecclesiastical authority than civil authority. The Catholic and Orthodox Church had, among others, a differing view of divorce.
</p><p>The Orthodox Church recognized that there are rare occasions when it is better that couples do separate. For the Orthodox, to say that marriage is indissoluble means that it should not be broken, the violation of such a union, perceived as holy, being an offense resulting from either adultery or the prolonged absence of one of the partners. Thus, permitting remarriage is an act of compassion of the Church towards sinful man.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the influence of the Catholic Church the divorce rate had been greatly reduced by the 9th or 10th century,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> which considered marriage a <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> instituted by <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> and indissoluble by mere human action.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although divorce, as known today, was generally prohibited in Catholic lands after the 10th century, separation of husband and wife and the <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> of marriage were well-known. What is today referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Separate_maintenance" class="mw-redirect" title="Separate maintenance">separate maintenance</a>" (or "<a href="/wiki/Legal_separation" title="Legal separation">legal separation</a>") was termed "divorce a mensa et thoro" ("divorce from bed-and-board"). The husband and wife physically separated and were forbidden to live or <a href="/wiki/Cohabit" class="mw-redirect" title="Cohabit">cohabit</a> together; but their marital relationship did not fully terminate.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Civil_courts" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil courts">Civil courts</a> had no power over marriage or divorce. The grounds for annulment were determined by a Catholic church authority and applied in <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_courts" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical courts">ecclesiastical courts</a>. Annulment was for <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canonical</a> causes of impediment existing at the time of the marriage. "For in cases of total divorce, the marriage is declared null, as having been absolutely unlawful ab initio."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The Catholic Church held that the sacrament of marriage produced one person from two, inseparable from each other: "By marriage the husband and wife are one person in law: that is, the very being of legal existence of the woman is suspended during the marriage or at least incorporated and consolidated into that of the husband: under whose wing, protection and cover, she performs everything."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Since husband and wife became one person upon marriage, recognition of that oneness could be rescinded only on the grounds that the unity never existed to begin with, <i>i.e.</i>, that the proclamation of marriage was erroneous and void <a href="/wiki/Ab_initio" title="Ab initio">from the start</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secularisation_in_Europe">Secularisation in Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Secularisation in Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1491_Henry_VIII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/1491_Henry_VIII.jpg/220px-1491_Henry_VIII.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/1491_Henry_VIII.jpg/330px-1491_Henry_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/1491_Henry_VIII.jpg/440px-1491_Henry_VIII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1213" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1491_Henry_VIII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> of England broke with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> in order to obtain an annulment.</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>, marriage came to be considered a <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contract</a> in the newly Protestant regions of Europe, and on that basis <a href="/wiki/Civil_authorities" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil authorities">civil authorities</a> gradually asserted their power to decree a "divortium a vinculo matrimonii", or "divorce from all the bonds of marriage".
</p><p>Since no <a href="/wiki/Precedents" class="mw-redirect" title="Precedents">precedents</a> existed defining the circumstances under which marriage could be dissolved, civil courts heavily relied on the previous determinations of the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastic">ecclesiastic</a> courts and freely adopted the requirements set down by those courts. As the civil courts assumed the power to dissolve marriages, courts still strictly construed the circumstances under which they would grant a divorce,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and considered divorce to be contrary to <a href="/wiki/Public_policy_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public policy (law)">public policy</a>. Because divorce was considered to be against the public interest, civil courts refused to grant a divorce if evidence revealed any hint of complicity between the husband and wife to divorce, or if they attempted to manufacture grounds for a divorce. Divorce was granted only because one party to the marriage had violated a sacred <a href="/wiki/Vow" title="Vow">vow</a> to the "innocent spouse". If both husband and wife were guilty, "neither would be allowed to escape the bonds of marriage".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually, the idea that a marriage could be dissolved in cases in which one of the parties violated the sacred vow gradually allowed expansion of the grounds upon which divorce could be granted from those grounds which existed at the time of the marriage to grounds which occurred after the marriage, but which exemplified violation of that vow, such as <a href="/wiki/Grounds_for_divorce_(United_States)#Abandonment_or_desertion" title="Grounds for divorce (United States)">abandonment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a>, or "extreme cruelty".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> An exception to this trend was the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a>, which maintained the doctrine of marital indissolubility.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> briefly passed a law that divested marriage of all sacrament, leaving it as a secular contract that could be broken. <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> wrote <a href="/wiki/Milton%27s_divorce_tracts" title="Milton&#39;s divorce tracts">four divorce tracts</a> in 1643–1645 that argued for the legitimacy of divorce on grounds of spousal incompatibility. His ideas were ahead of their time; arguing for divorce at all, let alone a version of <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault divorce</a>, was extremely controversial and religious figures sought to ban his tracts.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In 1670 a precedent was first set with an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> allowing Lord <a href="/wiki/John_Manners,_1st_Duke_of_Rutland" title="John Manners, 1st Duke of Rutland">John Manners</a> to divorce his wife, Lady Anne Pierrepont, and until the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Matrimonial_Causes_Act_1857" title="Matrimonial Causes Act 1857">Matrimonial Causes Act 1857</a>, divorce could only be obtained through a specific Act of Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_(Henri-Frederic_Schopin).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/230px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/345px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg/460px-Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_%28Henri-Frederic_Schopin%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="974" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_divorce_de_l%27Imp%C3%A9ratrice_Jos%C3%A9phine_15_d%C3%A9cembre_1809_(Henri-Frederic_Schopin).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais" class="mw-redirect" title="Joséphine de Beauharnais">Joséphine</a>, first wife of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, obtained the civil dissolution of her marriage under the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> of 1804.</div></div></div>
<p>The move towards secularisation and liberalisation was reinforced by the individualistic and secular ideals of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">Enlightened absolutist</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II_of_Prussia" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick II of Prussia">Frederick II</a> ("the Great") of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> decreed a new divorce law in 1752, in which marriage was declared to be a purely private concern, allowing divorce to be granted on the basis of mutual consent. This new attitude heavily influenced the law in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a> under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Joseph II</a>, where it was applied to all non-Catholic Imperial subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Divorce was legalised in France after the <a href="/wiki/French_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="French revolution">French revolution</a> on a similar basis, although the legal order of the <a href="/wiki/Ancien_regime" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancien regime">ancien regime</a> was reinstated at the <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourbon restoration">Bourbon restoration</a> of 1816. The trend in Europe throughout the 19th century, was one of increased liberalisation; by the mid-19th century divorce was generally granted by civil courts in the case of <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg/250px-Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg/375px-Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg/500px-Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marilyn_Monroe_and_Jerry_Giesler_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a> signing divorce papers with celebrity attorney <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Giesler" title="Jerry Giesler">Jerry Giesler</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In Britain before 1857 wives were regarded as under the economic and legal protection of their husbands, and divorce was almost impossible. It required a very expensive private Act of Parliament costing perhaps £200, of the sort only the richest could possibly afford. It was very difficult to secure divorce on the grounds of adultery, desertion, or cruelty. The first key legislative victory came with the <a href="/wiki/Matrimonial_Causes_Act_1857" title="Matrimonial Causes Act 1857">Matrimonial Causes Act 1857</a>, which passed over the strenuous opposition of the highly traditional Church of England. The new law made divorce a civil affair of the courts, rather than a Church matter, with a new civil court in London handling all cases. The process was still quite expensive, at about £40, but now became feasible for the middle class. A woman who obtained a judicial separation took the status of a <i>feme sole,</i> with full control of her own civil rights. Additional amendments came in 1878, which allowed for separations handled by local justices of the peace. The Church of England blocked further reforms until the final breakthrough came with the <a href="/wiki/Matrimonial_Causes_Act_1973" title="Matrimonial Causes Act 1973">Matrimonial Causes Act 1973</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the 1931 Constitution of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Second Spanish Republic</a> for the first time recognised a right to divorce. The first law to regulated divorce was the <i>Divorce Act of 1932</i>, that passed the Republican Parliament despite the opposition of the Catholic Church and a coalition of the Agrarian Minority and Minority Basque-Navarre Catholic parties. The dictatorship of General Franco abolished the law. After the restoration of democracy, a new divorce law was passed in 1981, again over the opposition of the Catholic Church and part of the Christian Democrat party, then a part of the ruling Union of Democratic Center. During the first socialist government of <a href="/wiki/Felipe_Gonz%C3%A1lez_M%C3%A1rquez" class="mw-redirect" title="Felipe González Márquez">Felipe González Márquez</a> the 1981 law was amended to expedite the process of separation and divorce of marriages, which was again opposed by the Church, which called it "express divorce".
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the first divorce law was introduced on 1 December 1970, despite the opposition of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Democrats">Christian Democrats</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and entered into force on 18 December 1970. In the following years, the Christian Democrats, supported also by parties opposed to the law, promoted a recall referendum. In 1974, in a referendum the majority of the population voted against a repeal of the divorce law. A feature of the 1970 divorce law was the long period of marital separation of five years required. This period was reduced to three in 1987 and to a year in 2015, in the case of judicial separation, and six months in the case of separation by mutual agreement.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> approved divorce at referenda in 1995 and 2011 respectively.
</p><p>Divorce rates increased markedly during the 20th century in developed countries, as social attitudes towards family and sex changed dramatically.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Divorce has become commonplace in some countries, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Haines_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haines-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan_2">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Edo Period (1603–1868) husbands could divorce their wives by writing letters of divorce. Frequently, their relatives or marriage arrangers kept these letters and tried to restore the marriages. Wives could not divorce their husbands. Some wives were able to gain sanctuary in certain Shinto "divorce temples".  After a wife had spent three years in a temple, her husband was required to divorce her.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In 19th century Japan, at least one in eight marriages ended in divorce.
</p><p>There are four types of divorce in Japan: divorce by agreement in which the divorce is mutual; divorce by mediation, which happens in family court; divorce by decision of family court that takes place when a couple cannot complete a divorce through mediation; and divorce by judgment of a district court.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India_2">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On an all-India level, the <a href="/wiki/Special_Marriage_Act,_1954" title="Special Marriage Act, 1954">Special Marriage Act</a> was passed in 1954, is an inter-religious marriage law permitting Indian nationals to marry and divorce irrespective of their religion or faith. The <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Marriage_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu Marriage Act">Hindu Marriage Act</a>, in 1955 which legally permitted divorce to Hindus and other communities who chose to marry under these acts. The Indian Divorce Act 1869<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> is the law relating to the divorce of person professing the Christian religion. Divorce can be sought by a husband or wife on grounds including adultery, cruelty, desertion for two years, religious conversion, mental abnormality, venereal disease, and leprosy.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Divorce is also available based on mutual consent of both the spouses, which can be filed after at least one year of separated living. Mutual consent divorce can not be appealed, and the law mandates a minimum period of six months (from the time divorce is applied for) for divorce to be granted.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Contested divorce is when one of the spouse is not willing to divorce the other spouse, under such condition the divorce is granted only on certain grounds according to the Hindu marriage act of 1955. While a Muslim husband can unilaterally bring an end to the marriage by pronouncing talaq,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Muslim women must go to court, claiming any of the grounds provided under the Dissolution of Muslim Marriage Act.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first major family law reform in the last decade, the Supreme Court of India banned the Islamic practice of "Triple Talaq" (divorce by uttering of the "Talaq" word thrice by the husband). The landmark Supreme Court of India judgment was welcomed by women activists across India.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Official figures of divorce rates are not available, but it has been estimated that 1 in 100 or another figure of 11 in 1,000 marriages in India end up in divorce.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various communities are governed by specific marital legislation, distinct to Hindu Marriage Act, and consequently have their own divorce laws:
</p>
<ul><li>The Parsi Marriage and Divorce Act, 1936<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Dissolution of Muslim Marriage act, 1939<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Foreign Marriage Act, 1969<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Muslim Women (Protection of Rights on Divorce) Act, 1986<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>An amendment to the marriage laws to allow divorce based on "irretrievable breakdown of marriage" (as alleged by one of the spouses) is under consideration in India.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In June 2010, the Union Cabinet of India approved the Marriage Laws (Amendment) Bill 2010, which, if cleared by Parliament, would establish "irretrievable breakdown" as a new ground for divorce.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Under the proposed amendment, the court before proceeding to the merits of the case must be satisfied by the evidences produced that parties have been living apart for a continuous period of not less than three years immediately preceding the presentation of the petition.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_law">Islamic law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Islamic law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_Islam" title="Divorce in Islam">Divorce in Islam</a></div>
<p>Divorce in Islam can take a variety of forms, some initiated by the husband and some initiated by the wife. The main traditional legal categories are <i>talaq</i> (repudiation), <i>khulʿ</i> (mutual divorce), judicial divorce and oaths. The theory and practice of divorce in the Islamic world have varied according to time and place.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-modern_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-modern-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Historically, the rules of divorce were governed by <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a>, as interpreted by <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">traditional Islamic jurisprudence</a>, and they differed depending on the <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">legal school</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-hist_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-hist-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Historical practice sometimes diverged from legal theory.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-hist_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-hist-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In modern times, as personal status (family) laws were codified, they generally remained "within the orbit of Islamic law", but control over the norms of divorce shifted from traditional jurists to the state.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-modern_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-modern-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Divorce as a means of terminating marriage is illegal for all Filipinos except <a href="/wiki/Filipino_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Filipino Muslim">Filipino Muslims</a>. There is only civil annulment after a lengthy legal separation. The process is costly and long, and there are many legally married couples in extramarital relations, even without a divorce law.
</p><p>Code of Muslim Personal Laws of the Philippines, known as Presidential Decree (PD) No. 1083, Title II- Marriage and Divorce, Chapter 3-Divorce allows for divorce recognized by the state. There are two <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> courts in the Philippine judicial system that hear these cases.
</p><p>On July 27, 2010, <a href="/wiki/GABRIELA" class="mw-redirect" title="GABRIELA">Gabriela Women's Party</a> filed in <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_the_Philippines" title="House of Representatives of the Philippines">Congress</a> House Bill No 1799, or the Divorce Bill of the Philippines, as one of many attempts to introduce pro-divorce legislation. Senator <a href="/wiki/Pia_Cayetano" title="Pia Cayetano">Pia Cayetano</a> has filed a separate divorce bill in the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Philippines" title="Senate of the Philippines">Senate</a>. During that time, the Philippines, along with Malta and the Vatican, are the three most conservative countries on the issue of divorce. The bill did not pass any level of legislation because of this.
</p><p>In 2013, the divorce bill was refiled, however, did not pass any level of legislation as well.
</p><p>In a latest attempt, the divorce bill was refiled again in 2017. On February 22, 2018, the House of Representatives committee on population and family relations approved a bill seeking to legalize divorce, the first time in Philippine history for such a measure to pass the committee level of legislation. The majority of the members of the House of Representatives (lower house of Congress), both majority and minority blocs, are in favor of divorce, however, divorce continues to be a divisive issue in the Senate (upper house of Congress), as stark opposition is present among male senators.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patterns">Patterns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Divorce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Patterns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Divorce rates increase during times of hardship, war, and major events. Divorce rates increased after World War II because people were quick to marry each other before they went to war. When soldiers returned, they found out that they didn't have much in common with their spouses, so they divorced.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_pol_en.htm">"European Commission - European Judicial Network - Divorce - Poland"</a>. <i>Europa.eu</i>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140924172930/http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_pol_en.htm">Archived</a> from the original on 2014-09-24<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2014-09-15</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Europa.eu&amp;rft.atitle=European+Commission+-+European+Judicial+Network+-+Divorce+-+Poland&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fec.europa.eu%2Fciviljustice%2Fdivorce%2Fdivorce_pol_en.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADivorce" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The marriage can be dissolved if it has broken down. The marriage has broken down if the parties to the marriage are no longer cohabiting and if it cannot be expected that the parties will resume matrimonial cohabitation (Section 1565 (1) BGB). There is an irrefutable presumption that the marriage has broken down if the parties have been living apart for one year and both apply for divorce or if the respondent consents to the divorce. After a separation period of three years, there is an irrefutable presumption that the marriage has broken down, without any comments being required from the parties to the proceedings (Section 1566 (2) BGB)" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_ger_en.htm">ec.europa.eu</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140924174728/http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_ger_en.htm">Archived</a> 2014-09-24 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Section 73 of the Civil Code explains the circumstances under which spouses can be considered to be living separately, namely, if the spouses do not share a household and one of the spouses refuses outright to maintain a joint household whereby the possibility of marital cohabitation is denied. Separate occupation by spouses of a common dwelling does not necessarily signify a joint household." (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_lat_en.htm">ec.europa.eu</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140924165742/http://ec.europa.eu/civiljustice/divorce/divorce_lat_en.htm">Archived</a> 2014-09-24 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>)</span>
</li>
